Conditional Debt Forgiveness When Death vs If Death
Ruling on conditional debt forgiveness as a will: valid with 'when I die', invalid with 'if I die'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a valid will that forgives a debt upon death ('when I die') and an invalid conditional statement ('if I die') that is considered a gamble.

Core Idea
A will that forgives a debt 'when I die' is valid; saying 'if I die' is a gamble and invalid.

The passage next discusses two similar phrasings for forgiving a debt in connection with death. One uses 'when I die' (idha muttu) and the other 'if I die' (in muttu). The former is a valid will; the latter is considered mukhatara (gambling) because death is certain, but 'if' introduces an unnecessary condition. This lesson analyzes the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two phrasings
The debtor says either 'When I die, you are free from the debt' or 'If I die, you are free from the debt.' These appear similar but have different legal consequences.
The first uses 'idha' (when), the second 'in' (if).
2
Understand the reasoning for validity
When the statement is 'when I die,' it is a conditional will where the condition (death) is certain. This is a valid suspension of the bequest upon death.
Death is inevitable, so the condition is not a gamble.
3
Understand the reasoning for invalidity
When the statement is 'if I die,' it resembles a conditional contract where the condition is uncertain. Death is certain, but phrasing it as 'if' implies a possibility that may not happen, making it akin to gambling (mukhatara).
The text equates it to 'If you enter the house, you are free from what you owe.'
4
Apply the ruling
Use 'idha muttu' for a valid will; avoid 'in muttu' as it is invalid. The creditor is considered to have made a will forgiving the debt upon his death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال أبرأت غرماني ولم يسمهم ولم ينو أحداً منهم بقلبه قال أبو القاسم: روى ابن مقاتل أصحابنا أنهم لا يبرؤون . رجل له دين على رجل فقال المديون إذا مت فأنت بريء من ذلك الدين قال أبو القاسم: يجوز ويكون وصية من الطالب للمطلوب. وفي النوازل سئل عن رجل كان له على رجل دين فقال له إذا مت فأنت بريء من ذلك الدين قال : يجوز وتكون وصية من الطالب للمطلوب إذا مات وإذا قال إن مت برئ من ذلك الدين قال : لا يبرأ وهو مخاطرة. وهو بمنزلة قوله إن دخلت الدار فأنت بريء مما عليك . وفي المنتقى : إذا قال الرجل ضعوا ثلثي حيث أمر الله تعالى يرد إلى الورثة. وفي الخلاصة: ولو قال ثلث مالي حيثما يرى الناس أو حيثما يرى المسلمون قيل في عرفنا ليست بوصية. وفي العيون: إذا قال انظروا إلى كل ما يجوز لي أن يوصى به فأعطوه فهذا على الثلث ولو قال انظروا ما يجوز لي أن أوصى به فأعطوه فالأمر إلى الورثة لأنه يجوز أن يوصي بدرهم وبأكثر. وقوله ما يجوز لي كذا ذكرهما ههنا ومراده إذا كانت الورثة كباراً ،كلهم أما إذا كان فيهم صغير أو من في معناه يجعل في حقه كان الموصي أوصى بدرهم لا غير لأنه هو المتيقن. وسئل أبو نصر عمن ادفعوا هذه الدراهم أو هذه الثياب إلى فلان ولم يقل هي له قال : إن هذا باطل لأن هذا ليس بوصية. وسئل أبو نصر الدبوسي عمن قال في وصيته ثلث مالي وقف ولم يزد على هذا قال : إن كان ماله نقداً يعني دراهم أو دنانير وما أشبه ذلك فهذا القول منه باطل وصار كقوله هذه الدراهم وقف، وإن كان ماله ضياعاً ونحوه صار وقفاً على الفقراء. وفي الظهيرية : وقد قيل الفتوى على أنه لا يجوز ما لم يبين جهة الوقف ولو أوصى رجل أن ما وجد مكتوباً من وصية والدي ولم أكن نفذتها تنفذ أو أقر بذلك على نفسه إقراراً في مرضه قالوا: هذه إن صدقته الورثة بتصديقهم، وإن كذبوه كان من الثلث بخلاف الدين. وفي الخانية : بخلاف الدين الذي لا طالب له إلا الله تعالى وكان حكمه حكم الزكاة والكفارات. وسئل محمد بن مقاتل عمن أوصى أن يعطي للناس ألف درهم قال : الوصية باطلة، ولو قال تصدقوا بألف درهم فهو جائز ويعطى للفقراء. وفي الخلاصة : لو قال لعبده أنت الله لا يعتق وقال محمد: الوصية جائزة وتصرف إلى وجوه البر وفي الخانية وفي مسألة العتق إن أراد به العتق عتق، وإن أراد به أنه الله لا يلزمه شيء والوصية تارة تكون بالألفاظ وتارة تكون بالإشارة المفهمة . قال في فتاوى أبي الليث : مريض أوصى وهو لا يقدر على الكلام لضعفه فأشار برأسه يعلم منه أنه يعتمد قال ابن مقاتل : تجوز وصيته عندي ولا تجوز عند أصحابنا، وكان الفقيه أبر الليث يقول : إذا فهم منه الإشارة يجوز. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : إذا كتب وصيته ثم قال أنفذوا وما في هذا الكتاب تنفذ وصيته ؛ هكذا ذكر في كتاب الشهادات قال الشيخ الإمام أبو وصية
And if he said: 'I have discharged my debtors' but did not name them and did not intend any of them in his heart, Abu al-Qasim said: Ibn Muqatil narrated from our companions that they are not discharged. A man had a debt upon another, and the debtor said: 'When I die, you are free from that debt.' Abu al-Qasim said: It is permitted and it becomes a will from the creditor for the debtor. In al-Nawazil, he was asked about a man who had a debt upon another and he said to him: 'When I die, you are free from that debt.' He said: It is permitted and it becomes a will from the creditor for the debtor when he dies. And if he said: 'If I die, you are free from that debt,' he said: He is not discharged, and it is a gamble (mukhatara). It is like his saying: 'If you enter the house, you are free from what you owe.' In al-Muntaqa: If a man said: 'Place my third where Allah commands,' it is returned to the heirs. In al-Khulasa: And if he said: 'My third is placed wherever people see or wherever Muslims see,' it is said in our custom that it is not a will. In al-‘Uyun: If he said: 'Look at everything permissible for me to bequeath and give it to him,' this is upon the third. And if he said: 'Look at what is permissible for me to bequeath and give it to him,' the matter is up to the heirs because it is permissible for him to will a dirham or more. And his saying 'what is permissible for me' — both were mentioned here; his intent is if all heirs are adults, but if there is a minor or similar among them, it is made in his case as if the testator willed one dirham only, because that is certain. And Abu Nasr was asked about one who said: 'Give these dirhams or these clothes to so-and-so' and did not say 'they are for him.' He said: This is invalid because it is not a will. And Abu Nasr al-Dabusi was asked about one who said in his will: 'My third is a waqf' and added nothing. He said: If his wealth is cash, i.e., dirhams or dinars and the like, then this statement of his is invalid and becomes like his saying 'these dirhams are a waqf'; and if his wealth is estates and the like, it becomes a waqf for the poor. In al-Zahiriyya: It has been said that the fatwa is that it is not permitted unless he specifies the direction of the waqf. And if a man willed that whatever is found written as the will of my father which I did not execute be executed, or he acknowledged that against himself in his illness, they said: This, if the heirs confirm it, it is by their confirmation, and if they deny it, it is from the third, unlike debt. In al-Khaniyya: Unlike a debt for which there is no claimant except Allah Almighty, and its ruling is like that of zakat and expiations. And Muhammad ibn Muqatil was asked about one who willed to give people a thousand dirhams. He said: The will is invalid. But if he said: 'Give charity with a thousand dirhams,' it is valid and given to the poor. In al-Khulasa: If he said to his slave: 'You are Allah,' he does not become free. And Muhammad said: The will is valid and it is given to good causes. And in al-Khaniyya, in the case of manumission: if he intended by it manumission, he is free; if he intended that he is Allah, nothing is binding upon him. And will sometimes is by words and sometimes by meaningful indication. It is stated in Fatawa Abi al-Layth: A sick person made a will while unable to speak due to weakness, and he indicated with his head, and it is known from him that he intended. Ibn Muqatil said: His will is valid according to me, but not according to our companions. And al-Faqih Abi al-Layth used to say: If the indication is understood from him, it is valid. And in Fatawa Abi al-Layth: If he wrote his will and then said: 'Execute, and whatever is in this book, execute my will.' Thus it is mentioned in the Book of Testimonies. The Shaykh Imam Abu... will
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a collection of miscellaneous rulings from various sources concerning the validity of different statements as wills. The first ruling clarifies that a general statement 'I have discharged my debtors' without naming them or specific intent does not effect a discharge; it is not a valid will. The second ruling addresses debt forgiveness linked to death: if the debtor says 'when I die, you are free from the debt,' it is a valid will from the creditor, i.e., the creditor is considered to have made a bequest forgiving the debt. But if the debtor says 'if I die, you are free,' it is considered a gamble (mukhatara) and invalid because it is conditional. The next ruling from al-Muntaqa: if someone says 'place my third where Allah commands' without specifying a charitable cause, the money is returned to the heirs because the direction is too vague. Similarly, saying 'wherever people see' is not a valid will. The 'Uyun ruling distinguishes between two similar phrases: 'look at everything permissible for me to bequeath' limits the bequest to one-third, while 'look at what is permissible for me to bequeath' gives discretion to the heirs, who may give less if they wish, unless there is a minor heir, in which case a minimum of one dirham is presumed. Abu Nasr rules that saying 'give these dirhams to so-and-so' without saying 'they are for him' does not constitute a will because ownership is not transferred. Al-Dabusi's ruling on making one-third a waqf without specifying the beneficiary: if the wealth is cash, the statement is invalid because cash waqf is not recognized in that context; if it is land, it becomes a waqf for the poor. The written father's will: if a person says to execute whatever is found written as his father's will, it is only binding if the heirs confirm; otherwise, it is from the one-third. The final rulings discuss the validity of a will by indication (ishara) and by writing. There is a difference of opinion: Ibn Muqatil allows indication if understood, while other Hanafi companions do not. Abu al-Layth accepts it. Writing a will and then instructing its execution is valid.

Key Terms
مخاطرة (mukhatara): Gambling or risk-taking; a prohibited element in contracts.
إذا (idha): When (conditional particle indicating certainty).
إن (in): If (conditional particle indicating possibility).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a debtor saying 'If I die, you are free from that debt'?
A) Valid will because death is certain.
B) Invalid because it is a gamble (mukhatara).
C) Valid only if the creditor agrees.
D) Invalid because a will cannot forgive debts.

Key Takeaway
A will forgiving a debt must be phrased with a certain condition ('when I die'), not an uncertain one ('if I die'), to avoid invalidity due to gambling.

Wasiyyah — Bequest

Vague Beneficiary Expressions in a Will: 'Where Allah Commands'
Ruling that vague expressions in a will are invalid; beneficiary must be specified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the validity of a will that uses vague expressions like 'where Allah commands' or 'wherever people see' based on Hanafi requirements of clarity.

Core Idea
A will that says 'place my third where Allah commands' without specifying a cause is invalid; the beneficiary must be clear.

The passage presents several cases where testators used vague directions such as 'where Allah commands' or 'wherever people see.' The ruling is that such expressions are not valid wills because they fail to identify the beneficiary or purpose. This lesson focuses on the need for specificity in charitable bequests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vague expression
"Place my third where Allah commands" is too vague. 'Where Allah commands' is not a specific charitable direction.
2
Understand the consequence
Because the direction is not specific, the will is invalid, and the money is returned to the heirs.
The text says 'yuraddu ila al-wuratha' (returned to the heirs).
3
Contrast with similar vague expressions
Saying 'wherever people see' or 'wherever Muslims see' is also invalid in the given custom.
The passage says 'fi urfina laysat bi-wasiyya' (in our custom it is not a will).

ولو قال أبرأت غرماني ولم يسمهم ولم ينو أحداً منهم بقلبه قال أبو القاسم: روى ابن مقاتل أصحابنا أنهم لا يبرؤون . رجل له دين على رجل فقال المديون إذا مت فأنت بريء من ذلك الدين قال أبو القاسم: يجوز ويكون وصية من الطالب للمطلوب. وفي النوازل سئل عن رجل كان له على رجل دين فقال له إذا مت فأنت بريء من ذلك الدين قال : يجوز وتكون وصية من الطالب للمطلوب إذا مات وإذا قال إن مت برئ من ذلك الدين قال : لا يبرأ وهو مخاطرة. وهو بمنزلة قوله إن دخلت الدار فأنت بريء مما عليك . وفي المنتقى : إذا قال الرجل ضعوا ثلثي حيث أمر الله تعالى يرد إلى الورثة. وفي الخلاصة: ولو قال ثلث مالي حيثما يرى الناس أو حيثما يرى المسلمون قيل في عرفنا ليست بوصية. وفي العيون: إذا قال انظروا إلى كل ما يجوز لي أن يوصى به فأعطوه فهذا على الثلث ولو قال انظروا ما يجوز لي أن أوصى به فأعطوه فالأمر إلى الورثة لأنه يجوز أن يوصي بدرهم وبأكثر. وقوله ما يجوز لي كذا ذكرهما ههنا ومراده إذا كانت الورثة كباراً ،كلهم أما إذا كان فيهم صغير أو من في معناه يجعل في حقه كان الموصي أوصى بدرهم لا غير لأنه هو المتيقن. وسئل أبو نصر عمن ادفعوا هذه Giving to a Person Without Saying 'It is for Him': Not a Will
Ruling that a direction to give without explicit ownership transfer is not a will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition of explicit ownership transfer in a will and apply it to determine the validity of a bequest.

Core Idea
Saying 'give these dirhams to so-and-so' without adding 'they are for him' does not transfer ownership; it is not a will.

Abu Nasr was asked about a person who said: 'Give these dirhams or these clothes to so-and-so' but did not say 'they are for him.' The ruling is that this is not a valid will because it does not clearly transfer ownership. This lesson highlights the need for explicit language of transfer in a bequest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the statement
The testator says: 'Give these dirhams to so-and-so' without adding 'they are for him.'
2
Identify the missing element
The phrase 'they are for him' (hiya lahu) is missing. This phrase is necessary to indicate ownership transfer.
Without it, the statement is merely a direction to give, not a bequest.
3
Apply the ruling
Abu Nasr rules that this is invalid (batiil) because it is not a will. The intended recipient does not gain ownership.
The text says 'inna hadha batil li-anna hadha laysa bi-wasiyya' (this is invalid because this is not a will).

أشبه ذلك فهذا القول منه باطل وصار كقوله هذه الدراهم وقف، وإن كان ماله ضياعاً ونحوه صار وقفاً على الفقراء. وفي الظهيرية : وقد قيل الفتوى على أنه لا يجوز ما لم يبين جهة الوقف ولو أوصى رجل أن ما وجد مكتوباً من وصية والدي ولم أكن نفذتها تنفذ أو أقر بذلك على نفسه إقراراً في مرضه قالوا: هذه إن صدقته الورثة بتصديقهم، وإن كذبوه كان من الثلث بخلاف الدين. وفي الخانية : بخلاف الدين الذي لا طالب له إلا الله تعالى وكان حكمه حكم الزكاة والكفارات. وسئل محمد بن مقاتل عمن أوصى أن يعطي للناس ألف درهم قال : الوصية باطلة، ولو قال تصدقوا بألف درهم فهو جائز ويعطى للفقراء. وفي الخلاصة : لو قال لعبده أنت الله لا يعتق وقال محمد: الوصية جائزة وتصرف إلى وجوه البر وفي الخانية وفي مسألة العتق إن أراد به العتق عتق، وإن أراد به أنه الله لا يلزمه شيء والوصية تارة تكون بالألفاظ وتارة تكون بالإشارة المفهمة . قال في فتاوى أبي الليث : مريض أوصى وهو لا يقدر على الكلام لضعفه فأشار برأسه يعلم منه أنه يعتمد قال ابن مقاتل : تجوز وصيته عندي ولا تجوز عند أصحابنا، وكان الفقيه أبر الليث يقول : إذا فهم منه الإشارة يجوز. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : إذا كتب وصيته ثم قال أنفذوا وما في هذا الكتاب تنفذ وصيته ؛ هكذا ذكر في كتاب الشهادات قال الشيخ الإمام أبو وصية
And if he said: 'I have discharged my debtors' but did not name them and did not intend any of them in his heart, Abu al-Qasim said: Ibn Muqatil narrated from our companions that they are not discharged. A man had a debt upon another, and the debtor said: 'When I die, you are free from that debt.' Abu al-Qasim said: It is permitted and it becomes a will from the creditor for the debtor. In al-Nawazil, he was asked about a man who had a debt upon another and he said to him: 'When I die, you are free from that debt.' He said: It is permitted and it becomes a will from the creditor for the debtor when he dies. And if he said: 'If I die, you are free from that debt,' he said: He is not discharged, and it is a gamble (mukhatara). It is like his saying: 'If you enter the house, you are free from what you owe.' In al-Muntaqa: If a man said: 'Place my third where Allah commands,' it is returned to the heirs. In al-Khulasa: And if he said: 'My third is placed wherever people see or wherever Muslims see,' it is said in our custom that it is not a will. In al-‘Uyun: If he said: 'Look at everything permissible for me to bequeath and give it to him,' this is upon the third. And if he said: 'Look at what is permissible for me to bequeath and give it to him,' the matter is up to the heirs because it is permissible for him to will a dirham or more. And his saying 'what is permissible for me' — both were mentioned here; his intent is if all heirs are adults, but if there is a minor or similar among them, it is made in his case as if the testator willed one dirham only, because that is certain. And Abu Nasr was asked about one who said: 'Give these dirhams or these clothes to so-and-so' and did not say 'they are for him.' He said: This is invalid because it is not a will. And Abu Nasr al-Dabusi was asked about one who said in his will: 'My third is a waqf' and added nothing. He said: If his wealth is cash, i.e., dirhams or dinars and the like, then this statement of his is invalid and becomes like his saying 'these dirhams are a waqf'; and if his wealth is estates and the like, it becomes a waqf for the poor. In al-Zahiriyya: It has been said that the fatwa is that it is not permitted unless he specifies the direction of the waqf. And if a man willed that whatever is found written as the will of my father which I did not execute be executed, or he acknowledged that against himself in his illness, they said: This, if the heirs confirm it, it is by their confirmation, and if they deny it, it is from the third, unlike debt. In al-Khaniyya: Unlike a debt for which there is no claimant except Allah Almighty, and its ruling is like that of zakat and expiations. And Muhammad ibn Muqatil was asked about one who willed to give people a thousand dirhams. He said: The will is invalid. But if he said: 'Give charity with a thousand dirhams,' it is valid and given to the poor. In al-Khulasa: If he said to his slave: 'You are Allah,' he does not become free. And Muhammad said: The will is valid and it is given to good causes. And in al-Khaniyya, in the case of manumission: if he intended by it manumission, he is free; if he intended that he is Allah, nothing is binding upon him. And will sometimes is by words and sometimes by meaningful indication. It is stated in Fatawa Abi al-Layth: A sick person made a will while unable to speak due to weakness, and he indicated with his head, and it is known from him that he intended. Ibn Muqatil said: His will is valid according to me, but not according to our companions. And al-Faqih Abi al-Layth used to say: If the indication is understood from him, it is valid. And in Fatawa Abi al-Layth: If he wrote his will and then said: 'Execute, and whatever is in this book, execute my will.' Thus it is mentioned in the Book of Testimonies. The Shaykh Imam Abu... will
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a collection of miscellaneous rulings from various sources concerning the validity of different statements as wills. The first ruling clarifies that a general statement 'I have discharged my debtors' without naming them or specific intent does not effect a discharge; it is not a valid will. The second ruling addresses debt forgiveness linked to death: if the debtor says 'when I die, you are free from the debt,' it is a valid will from the creditor, i.e., the creditor is considered to have made a bequest forgiving the debt. But if the debtor says 'if I die, you are free,' it is considered a gamble (mukhatara) and invalid because it is conditional. The next ruling from al-Muntaqa: if someone says 'place my third where Allah commands' without specifying a charitable cause, the money is returned to the heirs because the direction is too vague. Similarly, saying 'wherever people see' is not a valid will. The 'Uyun ruling distinguishes between two similar phrases: 'look at everything permissible for me to bequeath' limits the bequest to one-third, while 'look at what is permissible for me to bequeath' gives discretion to the heirs, who may give less if they wish, unless there is a minor heir, in which case a minimum of one dirham is presumed. Abu Nasr rules that saying 'give these dirhams to so-and-so' without saying 'they are for him' does not constitute a will because ownership is not transferred. Al-Dabusi's ruling on making one-third a waqf without specifying the beneficiary: if the wealth is cash, the statement is invalid because cash waqf is not recognized in that context; if it is land, it becomes a waqf for the poor. The written father's will: if a person says to execute whatever is found written as his father's will, it is only binding if the heirs confirm; otherwise, it is from the one-third. The final rulings discuss the validity of a will by indication (ishara) and by writing. There is a difference of opinion: Ibn Muqatil allows indication if understood, while other Hanafi companions do not. Abu al-Layth accepts it. Writing a will and then instructing its execution is valid.

Key Terms
هي له (hiya lahu): It is for him; phrase indicating ownership transfer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Nasr, what is the ruling if a person says 'Give these dirhams to so-and-so' without saying 'they are for him'?
A) Valid will because the intent is clear.
B) Invalid because it is not a will.
C) Valid but the recipient must accept.
D) Invalid because a will must be written.

Key Takeaway
A valid will must include explicit language transferring ownership; merely saying 'give' without 'it is for him' is insufficient.

Wasiyyah — Bequest

Vague Waqf in a Will: Cash vs Land
Ruling on vague waqf in a will: distinction between cash and land.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the distinction between cash and land when making a waqf in a will without specifying a beneficiary, and apply the ruling to other assets.

Core Idea
Making one-third a waqf without specifying the beneficiary: invalid for cash but valid for land (deemed for the poor).

Al-Dabusi was asked about a testator who said 'My third is a waqf' without adding any beneficiary. The ruling distinguishes between cash (dirhams/dinars) and land. For cash, the statement is invalid because cash waqf requires specification; for land, it becomes a valid waqf for the poor. This lesson explores the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the statement
The testator says: 'My third is a waqf' without specifying the beneficiary or the type of asset.
2
Classify the asset
Determine whether the wealth is cash (dirhams/dinars) or real estate (diya'/estates).
The classification is key.
3
Apply the ruling for cash
If the wealth is cash, the statement is invalid (batil) and is treated as if he said 'these dirhams are a waqf', which is not a valid waqf in Hanafi law because cash waqf is not recognized unless specified.
The text says 'hadha al-qawl minhu batil wa sar kaqawlihi hadhihi al-darahim waqf'.
4
Apply the ruling for land
If the wealth is land (estates), the statement becomes a valid waqf for the poor. The land is dedicated to charity.
The text says 'saara waqfan ala al-fuqara' (it becomes a waqf for the poor).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال أبرأت غرماني ولم يسمهم ولم ينو أحداً منهم بقلبه قال أبو القاسم: روى ابن مقاتل أصحابنا أنهم لا يبرؤون . رجل له دين على رجل فقال المديون إذا مت فأنت بريء من ذلك الدين قال أبو القاسم: يجوز ويكون وصية من الطالب للمطلوب. وفي النوازل سئل عن رجل كان له على رجل دين فقال له إذا مت فأنت بريء من ذلك الدين قال : يجوز وتكون وصية من الطالب للمطلوب إذا مات وإذا قال إن مت برئ من ذلك الدين قال : لا يبرأ وهو مخاطرة. وهو بمنزلة قوله إن دخلت الدار فأنت بريء مما عليك . وفي المنتقى : إذا قال الرجل ضعوا ثلثي حيث أمر الله تعالى يرد إلى الورثة. وفي الخلاصة: ولو قال ثلث مالي حيثما يرى الناس أو حيثما يرى المسلمون قيل في عرفنا ليست بوصية. وفي العيون: إذا قال انظروا إلى كل ما يجوز لي أن يوصى به فأعطوه فهذا على الثلث ولو قال انظروا ما يجوز لي أن أوصى به فأعطوه فالأمر إلى الورثة لأنه يجوز أن يوصي بدرهم وبأكثر. وقوله ما يجوز لي كذا ذكرهما ههنا ومراده إذا كانت الورثة كباراً ،كلهم أما إذا كان فيهم صغير أو من في معناه يجعل في حقه كان الموصي أوصى بدرهم لا غير لأنه هو المتيقن. وسئل أبو نصر عمن ادفعوا هذه الدراهم أو هذه الثياب إلى فلان ولم يقل هي له قال : إن هذا باطل لأن هذا ليس بوصية. وسئل أبو نصر الدبوسي عمن قال في وصيته ثلث مالي وقف ولم يزد على هذا قال : إن كان ماله نقداً يعني دراهم أو دنانير وما أشبه ذلك فهذا القول منه باطل وصار كقوله هذه الدراهم وقف، وإن كان ماله ضياعاً ونحوه صار وقفاً على الفقراء. وفي الظهيرية : وقد قيل الفتوى على أنه لا يجوز ما لم يبين جهة الوقف ولو أوصى رجل أن ما وجد الله تعالى وكان حكمه حكم الزكاة والكفارات. وسئل محمد بن مقاتل عمن أوصى أن يعطي للناس ألف درهم قال : الوصية باطلة، ولو قال تصدقوا بألف درهم فهو جائز ويعطى للفقراء. وفي الخلاصة : لو قال لعبده أنت الله نقد (naqd): Cash, money (dirhams, dinars).

Checkpoint
According to al-Dabusi, what is the ruling if a testator says 'My third is a waqf' and his wealth is cash?
A) It is a valid waqf for the poor.

C) It is valid only if the heirs agree.
D) It becomes a waqf for the testator's family.

Will by Indication (Ishara) and Writing
Rulings on will by indication and writing; intra-Hanafi difference of opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wills made by nonverbal indication and writing, and identify the difference of opinion on indication.

Core Idea
A will can be made by clear gesture or writing; later Hanafi authorities accept indication if understood, though Ibn Muqatil differs from earlier companions.

The passage concludes with a discussion of wills made by means other than speech. A sick person who cannot speak indicates his will by nodding his head. Ibn Muqatil allows it, while earlier companions did not. Abu al-Layth supports validity if the indication is understood. Additionally, writing a will and instructing its execution is valid. This lesson covers these modes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the modes of making a will
A will can be made by words (alfaz) or by meaningful indication (ishara mufhima) or by writing (kitaba).
2
Analyze the case of indication
A sick person unable to speak indicates his will by nodding his head. Ibn Muqatil says it is valid in his view, but not according to the majority of companions. Abu al-Layth says if the indication is understood, it is valid.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars.
3
Apply the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu al-Layth: if the indication is clearly understood, the will is valid.
This accommodates the needs of those unable to speak.
4
Consider writing
A written will, followed by an instruction to execute it, is valid. This is mentioned in the Book of Testimonies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أشبه ذلك فهذا القول منه باطل وصار كقوله هذه الدراهم وقف، وإن كان ماله ضياعاً ونحوه صار وقفاً على الفقراء. وفي الظهيرية : وقد قيل الفتوى على أنه لا يجوز ما لم يبين جهة الوقف ولو أوصى رجل أن ما وجد مكتوباً من وصية والدي ولم أكن نفذتها تنفذ أو أقر بذلك على نفسه إقراراً في مرضه قالوا: هذه إن صدقته الورثة بتصديقهم، وإن كذبوه كان من الثلث بخلاف الدين. وفي الخانية : بخلاف الدين الذي لا طالب له إلا الله تعالى وكان حكمه حكم الزكاة والكفارات. وسئل محمد بن مقاتل عمن أوصى أن يعطي للناس ألف درهم قال : الوصية باطلة، ولو قال تصدقوا بألف درهم فهو جائز ويعطى للفقراء. وفي الخلاصة : لو قال لعبده أنت الله لا يعتق وقال محمد: الوصية جائزة وتصرف إلى وجوه البر وفي الخانية وفي مسألة العتق إن أراد به العتق عتق، وإن أراد به أنه الله لا يلزمه شيء والوصية تارة تكون بالألفاظ وتارة تكون بالإشارة المفهمة . قال في فتاوى أبي الليث : مريض أوصى وهو لا يقدر على الكلام لضعفه فأشار برأسه يعلم منه أنه يعتمد قال ابن مقاتل : تجوز وصيته عندي ولا تجوز عند أصحابنا، وكان الفقيه أبر الليث يقول : إذا فهم منه الإشارة يجوز. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : إذا كتب وصيته ثم قال أنفذوا وما في هذا الكتاب تنفذ وصيته ؛ هكذا ذكر في كتاب الشهادات قال الشيخ الإمام أبو وصية
And if he said: 'I have discharged my debtors' but did not name them and did not intend any of them in his heart, Abu al-Qasim said: Ibn Muqatil narrated from our companions that they are not discharged. A man had a debt upon another, and the debtor said: 'When I die, you are free from that debt.' Abu al-Qasim said: It is permitted and it becomes a will from the creditor for the debtor. In al-Nawazil, he was asked about a man who had a debt upon another and he said to him: 'When I die, you are free from that debt.' He said: It is permitted and it becomes a will from the creditor for the debtor when he dies. And if he said: 'If I die, you are free from that debt,' he said: He is not discharged, and it is a gamble (mukhatara). It is like his saying: 'If you enter the house, you are free from what you owe.' In al-Muntaqa: If a man said: 'Place my third where Allah commands,' it is returned to the heirs. In al-Khulasa: And if he said: 'My third is placed wherever people see or wherever Muslims see,' it is said in our custom that it is not a will. In al-‘Uyun: If he said: 'Look at everything permissible for me to bequeath and give it to him,' this is upon the third. And if he said: 'Look at what is permissible for me to bequeath and give it to him,' the matter is up to the heirs because it is permissible for him to will a dirham or more. And his saying 'what is permissible for me' — both were mentioned here; his intent is if all heirs are adults, but if there is a minor or similar among them, it is made in his case as if the testator willed one dirham only, because that is certain. And Abu Nasr was asked about one who said: 'Give these dirhams or these clothes to so-and-so' and did not say 'they are for him.' He said: This is invalid because it is not a will. And Abu Nasr al-Dabusi was asked about one who said in his will: 'My third is a waqf' and added nothing. He said: If his wealth is cash, i.e., dirhams or dinars and the like, then this statement of his is invalid and becomes like his saying 'these dirhams are a waqf'; and if his wealth is estates and the like, it becomes a waqf for the poor. In al-Zahiriyya: It has been said that the fatwa is that it is not permitted unless he specifies the direction of the waqf. And if a man willed that whatever is found written as the will of my father which I did not execute be executed, or he acknowledged that against himself in his illness, they said: This, if the heirs confirm it, it is by their confirmation, and if they deny it, it is from the third, unlike debt. In al-Khaniyya: Unlike a debt for which there is no claimant except Allah Almighty, and its ruling is like that of zakat and expiations. And Muhammad ibn Muqatil was asked about one who willed to give people a thousand dirhams. He said: The will is invalid. But if he said: 'Give charity with a thousand dirhams,' it is valid and given to the poor. In al-Khulasa: If he said to his slave: 'You are Allah,' he does not become free. And Muhammad said: The will is valid and it is given to good causes. And in al-Khaniyya, in the case of manumission: if he intended by it manumission, he is free; if he intended that he is Allah, nothing is binding upon him. And will sometimes is by words and sometimes by meaningful indication. It is stated in Fatawa Abi al-Layth: A sick person made a will while unable to speak due to weakness, and he indicated with his head, and it is known from him that he intended. Ibn Muqatil said: His will is valid according to me, but not according to our companions. And al-Faqih Abi al-Layth used to say: If the indication is understood from him, it is valid. And in Fatawa Abi al-Layth: If he wrote his will and then said: 'Execute, and whatever is in this book, execute my will.' Thus it is mentioned in the Book of Testimonies. The Shaykh Imam Abu... will
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a collection of miscellaneous rulings from various sources concerning the validity of different statements as wills. The first ruling clarifies that a general statement 'I have discharged my debtors' without naming them or specific intent does not effect a discharge; it is not a valid will. The second ruling addresses debt forgiveness linked to death: if the debtor says 'when I die, you are free from the debt,' it is a valid will from the creditor, i.e., the creditor is considered to have made a bequest forgiving the debt. But if the debtor says 'if I die, you are free,' it is considered a gamble (mukhatara) and invalid because it is conditional. The next ruling from al-Muntaqa: if someone says 'place my third where Allah commands' without specifying a charitable cause, the money is returned to the heirs because the direction is too vague. Similarly, saying 'wherever people see' is not a valid will. The 'Uyun ruling distinguishes between two similar phrases: 'look at everything permissible for me to bequeath' limits the bequest to one-third, while 'look at what is permissible for me to bequeath' gives discretion to the heirs, who may give less if they wish, unless there is a minor heir, in which case a minimum of one dirham is presumed. Abu Nasr rules that saying 'give these dirhams to so-and-so' without saying 'they are for him' does not constitute a will because ownership is not transferred. Al-Dabusi's ruling on making one-third a waqf without specifying the beneficiary: if the wealth is cash, the statement is invalid because cash waqf is not recognized in that context; if it is land, it becomes a waqf for the poor. The written father's will: if a person says to execute whatever is found written as his father's will, it is only binding if the heirs confirm; otherwise, it is from the one-third. The final rulings discuss the validity of a will by indication (ishara) and by writing. There is a difference of opinion: Ibn Muqatil allows indication if understood, while other Hanafi companions do not. Abu al-Layth accepts it. Writing a will and then instructing its execution is valid.

Key Terms
إشارة مفهمة (ishara mufhima): A meaningful gesture that communicates intent clearly.
كتاب الشهادات (Kitab al-Shahadat): The Book of Testimonies, a section of Hanafi fiqh literature.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a will made by indication according to Abu al-Layth?
A) It is invalid because speech is required.
B) It is valid if the indication is understood.
C) It is valid only if written down.
D) It is invalid because Ibn Muqatil said it is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A will can be made by clear gesture or writing; the relied-upon Hanafi view accepts nonverbal indication if the intention is unmistakably understood.

Wills by Indication and Writing in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on wills by gesture and writing with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a will made by gesture or writing is valid according to the Hanafi mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A will can be made by clear gesture or writing if the intent is understood.

While spoken words are the primary mode of making a will, the Hanafi school recognizes situations where the testator cannot speak due to illness or other reasons. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the validity of wills made by meaningful indication (ishara mufhima) or in writing, presenting an intra-madhhab difference and the relied-upon fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modes of will
The passage states that a will can be made by words, by meaningful indication, or in writing. Recognize that the primary mode is spoken words, but alternatives are accepted under conditions.
2
Analyze the condition for indication
For a will by indication, the testator must be unable to speak (e.g., due to weakness) and the indication must be clearly understood. The passage gives the example of nodding the head.
3
Examine the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Ibn Muqatil accepted such a will if understood, but the majority of early Hanafi scholars (ashabuna) did not. Abu al-Layth later supported Ibn Muqatil's view, and his fatwa is relied upon.
4
Confirm the rule for written wills
A written will is valid if the testator writes it and then verbally instructs others to execute it (e.g., says 'Execute what is in this book').
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi is the position of Abu al-Layth: a will by clear indication is valid. For written wills, the instruction to execute is essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوصية تارة تكون بالألفاظ وتارة تكون بالإشارة المفهمة . قال في فتاوى أبي الليث : مريض أوصى وهو لا يقدر على الكلام لضعفه فأشار برأسه يعلم منه أنه يعتمد قال ابن مقاتل : تجوز وصيته عندي ولا تجوز عند أصحابنا، وكان الفقيه أبر الليث يقول : إذا فهم منه الإشارة يجوز. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : إذا كتب وصيته ثم قال أنفذوا وما في هذا الكتاب تنفذ وصيته ؛ هكذا ذكر في كتاب الشهادات قال الشيخ الإمام أبو
And will sometimes is by words and sometimes by meaningful indication. It is stated in Fatawa Abi al-Layth: A sick person made a will while unable to speak due to weakness, and he indicated with his head, and it is known from him that he intended. Ibn Muqatil said: His will is valid according to me, but not according to our companions. And al-Faqih Abi al-Layth used to say: If the indication is understood from him, it is valid. And in Fatawa Abi al-Layth: If he wrote his will and then said: 'Execute, and whatever is in this book, execute my will.' Thus it is mentioned in the Book of Testimonies. The Shaykh Imam Abu...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage specifically addresses the modes of making a will beyond spoken words. The general principle is that a will can be made by words (alfaz) or by meaningful indication (ishara mufhima). The text then presents a difference of opinion regarding a person who cannot speak due to illness and indicates by nodding his head. Ibn Muqatil considers such a will valid if the intention is understood, but the majority of Hanafi scholars (our companions, ashabuna) do not accept it. However, Abu al-Layth supports validity if the indication is clearly understood. The passage also confirms the validity of a written will: if a person writes his will and then instructs others to execute it, it is a valid will. This is referenced from the Book of Testimonies (Kitab al-Shahadat). The text cuts off near the end of the page, likely continuing with the name of the shaykh.

Key Terms
إشارة مفهمة (ishara mufhima): A clear gesture that conveys meaning, admissible for making a will when unable to speak.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Muqatil, is a will made by a sick person through a head gesture valid?
A) Yes, if the gesture is understood.
B) No, only spoken words are valid.
C) Yes, but only if written down afterwards.
D) No, because the majority of Hanafi scholars reject it.

Key Takeaway
A will is valid when made by clear gesture or writing with intent, according to the relied-upon Hanafi fatwa.

Will During Illness: Conditional on Death
Stating ruling on validity of will when testator recovers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a will made during illness when the testator recovers, and identify the condition that determines its validity.

Core Idea
A will made during a fatal illness is contingent upon death; recovery annuls it.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Rai'iq clarifies a critical point in Hanafi inheritance law: the status of a will made during a terminal illness. While such wills are generally valid, they are inherently tied to the illness that prompted them. The key factor is whether the testator's recovery occurs; if so, the will loses its effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is making a will (wasiyyah) during a fatal illness. The default ruling is that such wills are valid (sahih) provided they do not exceed one-third of the estate.
2
Recognize the conditional phrasing
If the testator explicitly conditions the will on dying from that illness (e.g., 'If I die from this illness, give X to Y') or on not recovering, the will becomes contingent.
The condition may be expressed in Arabic or Persian.
3
Determine the consequence of recovery
If the testator recovers from the illness, the condition fails and the will becomes void (batalat). The will is not reinstated upon a later death.
4
Apply to unconditional wills
If the testator made a will during illness without any condition linking it to that illness, it remains valid even if he recovers.
The passage's default rule is that wills made during illness are valid; the exception is conditional ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ثم مرض فوصاياه ثابتة إن لم يقل إن مت من مرضي هذا أو قال إن لم أبرأ من مرضي هذا فقد أوصيت بكذا أو قال بالفارسية الدمن ارين سماري غير من حينئذ إذا برأ بطلت وصيته.
He said: Then he fell ill, and his wills are valid unless he said 'If I die from this illness' or said 'If I do not recover from this illness, I have bequeathed such-and-such' or said in Persian 'if I recover' – then if he recovers, his will is void.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a ruling about the validity of a will made during a fatal illness (marad al-mawt). The principle is that such a will is contingent upon death from that illness. If the testator phrases the will conditionally (e.g., 'If I die from this illness, then so-and-so gets X'), and he recovers, the will is automatically revoked. The ruling covers various phrasings in Arabic and Persian. The key point: the will is linked to the illness; recovery annuls it.

Key Terms
وصايا (wasaya): Plural of wasiyyah; bequests or wills made by a person.
مرض (marad): Illness; specifically marad al-mawt (deathbed illness) which triggers legal restrictions on transactions.
برأ (bari'a): Recovery; restoration to health after illness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person on his deathbed says, 'If I die from this illness, give my car to Zaid,' and then recovers, what happens to the will?
A) The will remains valid and must be carried out after his eventual death.
B) The will becomes void because he did not die from that illness.
C) The will becomes a general will applicable after any death.
D) The will is void ab initio because it was conditional.

Key Takeaway
A will made during a fatal illness and conditioned on death from that illness becomes void if the testator recovers.

Discharge of Debts in a Will: Specification and Prohibition of Gambling
Rulings on testator discharging debtors without naming and conditional discharge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid forms of discharging debts in a will, and apply the Hanafi requirement of specification and prohibition of gambling.

Core Idea
A vague discharge of debtors is invalid; a conditional discharge is valid only if stated by the creditor, not the debtor.

In the Hanafi school, a will (wasiyyah) to forgive debts is valid only if the testator specifies the debtor or intends a particular person. Moreover, discharging a debt conditionally upon an uncertain event, like death, is considered gambling (mukhatarah) if the debtor makes the condition, but valid as a will if the creditor makes it. This lesson unpacks these two principles from the text of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two distinct rulings: one about a vague statement ('I have discharged my debtors') and another about a conditional discharge ('If I die, you are free'). Isolate each scenario.
2
Determine the reason for invalidity in the first scenario
Analyze why the vague discharge is ineffective: lack of specification (naming or intention) renders the will ambiguous and unenforceable.
Hanafis require the testator to know who the debtor is.
3
Analyze the conditional discharge
Distinguish between the creditor making the condition (valid will) and the debtor making it (invalid gamble). Understand why the debtor's condition is mukhatarah.
Conditioning forgiveness on death is like a wager on an uncertain event.
4
Connect to the broader prohibition of gambling
Relate mukhatarah to the general prohibition of qimar (gambling) in Islamic law, emphasizing that uncertainty (gharar) in transactions is impermissible.
5
Extract the legal maxim
Formulate a rule: 'A will to discharge a debt is valid only if the debtor is specified; a conditional discharge is valid only if the condition is made by the creditor.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال أبرأت غرماني ولم يسمهم ولم ينو أحداً منهم بقلبه قال أبو القاسم: روى ابن مقاتل أصحابنا أنهم لا يبرؤون . رجل له دين على رجل فقال المديون إذا مت فأنت بريء من ذلك الدين قال أبو القاسم: يجوز ويكون وصية من الطالب للمطلوب. وفي النوازل سئل عن رجل كان له على رجل دين فقال له إذا مت فأنت بريء من ذلك الدين قال : يجوز وتكون وصية من الطالب للمطلوب إذا مات وإذا قال إن مت برئ من ذلك الدين قال : لا يبرأ وهو مخاطرة. وهو بمنزلة قوله إن دخلت الدار فأنت بريء مما عليك .
And if he said 'I have discharged my debtors' and did not name them and did not intend any of them in his heart, Abu al-Qasim said: Ibn Muqatil narrated from our companions that they are not discharged. A man had a debt against another man, and the debtor said 'If I die, you are free from that debt.' Abu al-Qasim said: It is permissible and it is a will from the claimant (creditor) to the debtor. And in al-Nawazil, he was asked about a man who had a debt against another man and said to him 'If I die, you are free from that debt.' He said: It is permissible and it is a will from the claimant to the debtor when he dies. And if he said 'If I die, I am free from that debt,' he said: He is not free, and it is a gamble (mukhatarah). And it is like his saying 'If you enter the house, you are free from what you owe.'
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage covers two scenarios. First, if a testator says 'I have discharged my debtors' without specifying names or intending anyone, the debtors are not discharged because the statement is too vague to be a valid will. Second, if a debtor says to his creditor 'If I die, you are free from the debt' (i.e., the debt is waived upon the debtor's death), this is a valid will from the creditor (the one owed) because he is forgiving the debt after his death, which falls under a will. However, if the debtor says 'If I die, I am free from the debt' (i.e., the debt is cancelled), it is not valid because it is a gamble (mukhatarah) – the debtor is betting on death, which is uncertain. The same applies to conditional discharge like 'If you enter the house, you are free from what you owe.'

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra'): Discharge or waiver of a debt; freeing the debtor from liability.
مخاطرة (mukhatarah): Gamble; an uncertain transaction where outcome is contingent on chance.
غرماني (ghuramai): My debtors; those who owe me debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a debtor says to his creditor, 'If I die, I am free from the debt,' what is the ruling on the discharge?
A) The debt is discharged upon the debtor's death.
B) The discharge is valid as a will from the creditor.
C) The discharge is invalid because it is a gamble (mukhatarah).
D) The discharge is valid if the creditor agrees.

Key Takeaway
A will discharging a debt requires specification of the debtor; conditional discharge is valid only when stated by the creditor, not the debtor, to avoid gambling.

Vague Expressions in Wills: The Hanafi Requirement of Clarity
Rulings on testator using vague terms like 'where Allah commands' or 'as people see fit'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three rulings for vague bequests and apply the principle that ambiguity invalidates or refers the matter to heirs.

Core Idea
A vague will is no will — the testator must specify a valid beneficiary or purpose.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines three common vague phrases testators use when bequeathing their one-third. The Hanafi school insists that a will must clearly identify the beneficiary or charitable purpose; otherwise, the estate reverts to the heirs. We will explore each case and understand the reasoning, including the special consideration when minor heirs are involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vague phrase
Locate the three phrases: 'where Allah commands', 'wherever people see fit', and 'look at what is permissible for me to bequeath'. Each lacks a specific beneficiary.
The testator uses ambiguous language, not naming a person or explicit cause.
2
Apply the condition of clarity
Hanafi law requires the will's object (beneficiary) to be known. If the phrase fails to designate a clear recipient, the will is invalid or its execution is suspended.
Clarity (wuduh) is a condition for validity.
3
Determine the ruling
For the first two phrases, the will is void and the one-third returns to the heirs. For the third phrase, if heirs are all adults, they decide; if a minor exists, only a dirham is considered bequeathed.
The ruling varies based on the phrase and the presence of minors.
4
Analyze the exception for minors
When a minor heir exists, the court presumes the minimum possible amount (a dirham) because that is the certain amount (al-mutayaqqan) the testator could have intended.
This protects the minor's inheritance from uncertainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى : إذا قال الرجل ضعوا ثلثي حيث أمر الله تعالى يرد إلى الورثة. وفي الخلاصة: ولو قال ثلث مالي حيثما يرى الناس أو حيثما يرى المسلمون قيل في عرفنا ليست بوصية. وفي العيون: إذا قال انظروا إلى كل ما يجوز لي أن يوصى به فأعطوه فهذا على الثلث ولو قال انظروا ما يجوز لي أن أوصى به فأعطوه فالأمر إلى الورثة لأنه يجوز أن يوصي بدرهم وبأكثر. وقوله ما يجوز لي كذا ذكرهما ههنا ومراده إذا كانت الورثة كباراً ،كلهم أما إذا كان فيهم صغير أو من في معناه يجعل في حقه كان الموصي أوصى بدرهم لا غير لأنه هو المتيقن.
And in al-Muntaqa: If a man says 'Place my one-third where Allah commands,' it is returned to the heirs. And in al-Khulasa: If he says 'My one-third wherever people see fit' or 'wherever Muslims see fit,' it was said that in our custom it is not a will. And in al-Uyun: If he says 'Look at everything that is permissible for me to bequeath and give it,' then this is from the one-third. And if he says 'Look at what is permissible for me to bequeath and give it,' then the matter is up to the heirs, because it is permissible for him to bequeath a dirham or more. And his saying 'what is permissible for me' – he mentioned both here, and his intention is when the heirs are all adults. But if there is a minor among them or someone in his position, then regarding him, it is as if the testator bequeathed a dirham only, because that is the certain amount.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage discusses three cases where the testator uses vague language regarding his one-third. First, saying 'place my third where Allah commands' is too vague; the estate goes to the heirs. Second, 'wherever people see fit' is not considered a valid will in our custom because it lacks direction. Third, if he says 'give whatever is permissible for me to bequeath,' this is ambiguous. If the heirs are all adults, the decision is left to them. But if there is a minor, then only a dirham is considered bequeathed, because that is the minimum certain amount the testator could have intended.

Key Terms
حيث أمر الله (haythu amara Allah): Where Allah commands; a vague reference to charitable causes.
الورثة (al-warathah): The legal heirs entitled to inherit.
المتيقن (al-mutayaqqan): The certain amount; what is definitely intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A testator says, 'Give my one-third wherever Muslims see fit.' What is the ruling?
A) The will is valid and must be distributed to Muslims.
B) The will is valid but only for poor Muslims.
C) It is not considered a will in our custom.
D) It is decided by the ruler.

Key Takeaway
For a will to be valid, the testator must clearly identify the beneficiary or purpose; otherwise, the bequest is void or left to the heirs, and if a minor heir exists, only the minimum certain amount (a dirham) is effective.

Ambiguous Instructions in Wills: Cash vs. Land
Rulings on vague commands like 'give these dirhams' without specifying ownership or purpose

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid ambiguous statements in a will according to Hanafi fiqh, specifically regarding cash and land.

Core Idea
A vague instruction to 'give these dirhams' without ownership is not a will; a general 'my one-third is waqf' is void for cash but valid for land.

The passage presents two rulings from Abu Nasr and Abu Nasr al-Dabusi on ambiguous testamentary instructions. These rulings illustrate that the validity of such instructions depends on the clarity of intent and the nature of the property. Understanding these nuances is crucial for drafting valid wills in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two ambiguous statements
Read the passage and identify the two separate instructions: 'Give these dirhams/garments to so-and-so' (without stating ownership) and 'My one-third is waqf' (without specifying beneficiary).
These are two distinct cases: one is a potential gift, the other a potential endowment.
2
Analyze the first ruling: gift without ownership
The first statement is invalid because a will requires a clear transfer of ownership. Merely instructing 'give' without stating 'it is for him' does not constitute a bequest (wasiyyah).
Ownership must be explicitly or implicitly clear; here it is ambiguous.
3
Analyze the second ruling: waqf without specification
For the waqf instruction, the ruling depends on the type of property. If the wealth is cash (naqd), the statement is void because cash cannot be made waqf without specifying the purpose. If it is land (diya'), it becomes a valid waqf for the poor.
The difference is based on the nature of the asset: land can be dedicated generally, cash cannot.
4
Apply the rulings to a scenario
Consider a testator with a mixed estate of cash and land who says 'my one-third is waqf'. Determine that the land portion becomes waqf for the poor, while the cash portion is void and remains part of the estate for heirs.
This distinction is critical for executors and judges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسئل أبو نصر عمن ادفعوا هذه الدراهم أو هذه الثياب إلى فلان ولم يقل هي له قال : إن هذا باطل لأن هذا ليس بوصية. وسئل أبو نصر الدبوسي عمن قال في وصيته ثلث مالي وقف ولم يزد على هذا قال : إن كان ماله نقداً يعني دراهم أو دنانير وما أشبه ذلك فهذا القول منه باطل وصار كقوله هذه الدراهم وقف، وإن كان ماله ضياعاً ونحوه صار وقفاً على الفقراء.
And Abu Nasr was asked about someone who said 'Give these dirhams or these garments to so-and-so' and did not say 'they are for him.' He said: This is invalid because this is not a will. And Abu Nasr al-Dabusi was asked about someone who said in his will 'My one-third is waqf' and did not add more. He said: If his wealth is cash, meaning dirhams or dinars and the like, then this statement of his is void and it is like his saying 'These dirhams are waqf.' And if his wealth is estates and the like, it becomes waqf for the poor.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings on ambiguous instructions. First, if a testator says 'give these dirhams to so-and-so' without stating 'they are for him' (i.e., ownership transfer), the instruction is invalid because it does not constitute a will – a will requires clear transfer of ownership. Second, if the testator says 'my one-third is waqf' without specifying the beneficiary: if the estate consists of cash (dirhams/dinars), the statement is void because cash cannot be made waqf in perpetuity without specification (according to Hanafi law). But if the estate consists of land (diya'), it becomes a waqf for the poor, as land can be made waqf generally.

Key Terms
وقف (waqf): Endowment; alienation of property for charitable purposes, with the corpus retained.
نقد (naqd): Cash; dirhams and dinars.
ضياع (diya'): Estates; agricultural lands or real property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A testator says in his will: 'My one-third is waqf' without specifying for whom. His wealth consists of 10,000 dirhams. What is the ruling?
A) The waqf is valid and the money is given to the poor.
B) The statement is void because cash cannot be made waqf without specification.
C) The waqf is valid and the heirs decide the beneficiary.
D) The money is divided among the poor.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, ambiguous testamentary instructions are invalid for cash waqf without specification, but valid for land waqf; and 'give without ownership' is not a valid will.

Executing a Found Written Will of One's Father
Ruling on implementing a will found written by the testator's father

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on executing a father's written will found by the testator, and explain the difference in treatment between a bequest and a debt when heirs deny it.

Core Idea
A found father's will is executed only if heirs confirm; otherwise limited to one-third of estate.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi wasiyyah law: a testator instructs that any written will of his father that he found but did not execute should now be executed. The Hanafi scholars rule based on the heirs' confirmation or denial, and they distinguish this situation from a debt, which would be paid from the entire estate regardless of denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The testator declares in his will that whatever written will of his father was found and not executed should be executed, or he acknowledges its existence during his illness.
This is a conditional bequest (wasiyyah) regarding an existing document.
2
Determine the two outcomes based on heirs' response
If the heirs confirm (tasdiq) the existence of the father's will, it is executed from the testator's estate. If they deny it, the bequest is limited to one-third (al-thuluth) of the testator's estate.
The one-third limit is the general rule for wills in Hanafi law.
3
Note the contrast with debt
The passage explicitly distinguishes this from a debt (dayn). A debt—even one owed to Allah like unpaid zakah or kaffarat—is paid from the entire estate, without the one-third limit, and does not require heirs' confirmation.
Zakah and kaffarat are mentioned as examples of debts with no human claimant.
4
Understand the reasoning behind the distinction
A father's will is not a debt of the testator; it is a bequest that he is fulfilling on behalf of his father. Since it is a voluntary act, it is subject to the usual wasiyyah rules. A debt, however, is a binding obligation that must be fulfilled from the estate before bequests.
This highlights the Hanafi principle that debts take priority over bequests.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : وقد قيل الفتوى على أنه لا يجوز ما لم يبين جهة الوقف ولو أوصى رجل أن ما وجد مكتوباً من وصية والدي ولم أكن نفذتها تنفذ أو أقر بذلك على نفسه إقراراً في مرضه قالوا: هذه إن صدقته الورثة بتصديقهم، وإن كذبوه كان من الثلث بخلاف الدين. وفي الخانية : بخلاف الدين الذي لا طالب له إلا الله تعالى وكان حكمه حكم الزكاة والكفارات.
And in al-Zahiriyyah: And it has been said that the fatwa is that it is not permissible unless he specifies the direction of the waqf. And if a man bequeaths that 'whatever is found written from my father's will that I did not execute is to be executed' or he acknowledges that against himself during his illness, they said: This is valid if the heirs confirm it by their confirmation, but if they deny it, it is from the one-third, contrary to debt. And in al-Khaniyyah: Contrary to debt, which has no claimant except Allah Almighty, and its ruling is like the ruling of zakah and expiations.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage from al-Zahiriyyah deals with a testator who instructs that any written will of his father that he did not execute should now be executed, or he acknowledges that such a will exists. The ruling: if the heirs confirm the existence of the father's will, then it is executed from the entire estate? Actually, the text says 'these are valid if the heirs confirm'. If the heirs deny it, then the bequest is limited to one-third of the testator's estate, which is the general rule for wills. This differs from a debt (like a debt owed by the testator), which is paid from the entire estate without limit. The reference to zakah and kaffarat indicates that debts owed to Allah (like unpaid zakah) are treated similarly to human debts? But the text says 'contrary to debt' meaning the father's will is like a debt? Actually careful: 'bikhilafi al-dayn' – it is different from debt, meaning if it were a debt (to Allah), it would be from the entire estate, but here it is from the third.

Key Terms
تصديق (tasdiq): Confirmation or acknowledgment by the heirs.
الثلث (al-thuluth): One-third of the estate; the maximum limit for wills in Hanafi law.
الدين (al-dayn): Debt; a financial obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person says in his will: 'Execute whatever written will of my father I found but did not execute.' The heirs deny the existence of such a will. What happens?
A) The will is ignored completely.
B) The will is executed from the entire estate.
C) The will is executed from one-third of the testator's estate.
D) The will is executed only if the testator's father had witnesses.

Key Takeaway
A found father's will is executed only if heirs confirm; otherwise it is limited to one-third of the estate, unlike a debt which is paid in full.

Vague Bequest: 'Give to the People' vs. 'Give Charity'
Ruling on vague bequest to 'people' vs. charity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid wording in a will for charitable disbursement.

Core Idea
A bequest to 'the people' is void; 'give charity' is valid and given to the poor.

The Hanafi school requires specificity in the beneficiary of a will. The vague term 'people' fails to identify a valid recipient, rendering the will void. However, if the testator uses the word 'charity,' the will is valid and the funds must be given to the poor, as they are the primary recipients of charity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two expressions
Compare 'give to the people' vs. 'give charity' used in the will.
The first is vague, the second is specific in purpose.
2
Note the ruling
The will with 'people' is void; the will with 'charity' is valid.
Void means the bequest fails and the amount returns to the estate.
3
Understand the basis
Vague beneficiary invalidates, but 'charity' implicitly designates the poor as a recognized category.
Hanafi law requires the beneficiary to be specified or fall under a known charitable class.
4
Recognize the practical implication
For a valid will, either name individuals or specify a category like 'the poor' or 'the needy.'
Avoid terms like 'people', 'humanity', or 'everyone'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسئل محمد بن مقاتل عمن أوصى أن يعطي للناس ألف درهم قال : الوصية باطلة، ولو قال تصدقوا بألف درهم فهو جائز ويعطى للفقراء. وفي الخلاصة : لو قال لعبده أنت الله لا يعتق وقال محمد: الوصية جائزة وتصرف إلى وجوه البر وفي الخانية وفي مسألة العتق إن أراد به العتق عتق، وإن أراد به أنه الله لا يلزمه شيء
And Muhammad ibn Muqatil was asked about someone who bequeathed to give a thousand dirhams to 'the people.' He said: The will is void. And if he said 'Give charity with a thousand dirhams,' it is permissible and is given to the poor. And in al-Khulasa: If he said to his slave 'You are Allah' – he is not free. And Muhammad said: The will is permissible and is directed to charitable purposes. And in al-Khaniyyah: And in the issue of manumission, if he intended thereby manumission, he becomes free; and if he intended thereby that he is Allah, nothing is incumbent upon him.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage covers two scenarios. First, bequeathing to 'the people' (an-nas) is void because it is too vague; the beneficiaries are unspecified. However, if he says 'give charity with a thousand dirhams,' it is valid and the money goes to the poor. Second, a testator says to his slave 'You are Allah' (a phrase that could be a statement of manumission or a blasphemous utterance). According to Muhammad (al-Shaybani?), the will is valid and the slave's value (if intended as manumission) is part of the will. But in al-Khaniyyah, it clarifies: if he intended manumission, the slave is freed (as part of the will from the one-third); if he intended literally 'you are Allah,' then nothing is required because it is meaningless. The key is the testator's intention.

Key Terms
الناس (al-nas): The people; a vague term in wills that does not designate a valid recipient.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A testator says in his will: 'Give one thousand dirhams to the people.' What is the ruling?
A) The will is valid and must be distributed among all people.
B) The will is void because the beneficiaries are unspecified.
C) The will is valid but only for the poor.
D) The will is valid and the heirs decide the distribution.

Key Takeaway
A bequest to a vague group like 'the people' is void; specifying 'charity' or a category like 'the poor' makes the will valid.

Ambiguous Manumission: 'You are Allah' in a Will
Ruling on ambiguous manumission phrase in will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the validity of a will based on the testator's intention when using ambiguous manumission expressions.

Core Idea
If the testator says to his slave 'You are Allah' intending manumission, the slave is freed; otherwise, the statement is meaningless.

In the Hanafi school, the intention of the testator is crucial when interpreting ambiguous phrases in a will. The phrase 'أنت الله' (You are Allah) could be a statement of manumission or a meaningless utterance. The ruling depends on the testator's intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression
The phrase 'You are Allah' said to a slave in the context of a will.
This phrase is ambiguous; it may be intended as manumission or something else.
2
Note the default ruling
According to al-Khulasa, the slave is not freed by the mere utterance; intention matters.
The will itself is valid (the slave's value is a bequest).
3
Determine the two possibilities
If the testator intended manumission, the slave is freed from the one-third. If he intended the literal meaning (blasphemy), nothing is required.
The testator's intention is inferred from context and evidence.
4
Apply the principle
In Hanafi law, ambiguous expressions in wills are interpreted according to the testator's true intent.
This reflects the broader rule: 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (actions are judged by intentions).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسئل محمد بن مقاتل عمن أوصى أن يعطي للناس ألف درهم قال : الوصية باطلة، ولو قال تصدقوا بألف درهم فهو جائز ويعطى للفقراء. وفي الخلاصة : لو قال لعبده أنت الله لا يعتق وقال محمد: الوصية جائزة وتصرف إلى وجوه البر وفي الخانية وفي مسألة العتق إن أراد به العتق عتق، وإن أراد به أنه الله لا يلزمه شيء
And Muhammad ibn Muqatil was asked about someone who bequeathed to give a thousand dirhams to 'the people.' He said: The will is void. And if he said 'Give charity with a thousand dirhams,' it is permissible and is given to the poor. And in al-Khulasa: If he said to his slave 'You are Allah' – he is not free. And Muhammad said: The will is permissible and is directed to charitable purposes. And in al-Khaniyyah: And in the issue of manumission, if he intended thereby manumission, he becomes free; and if he intended thereby that he is Allah, nothing is incumbent upon him.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage covers two scenarios. First, bequeathing to 'the people' (an-nas) is void because it is too vague; the beneficiaries are unspecified. However, if he says 'give charity with a thousand dirhams,' it is valid and the money goes to the poor. Second, a testator says to his slave 'You are Allah' (a phrase that could be a statement of manumission or a blasphemous utterance). According to Muhammad (al-Shaybani?), the will is valid and the slave's value (if intended as manumission) is part of the will. But in al-Khaniyyah, it clarifies: if he intended manumission, the slave is freed (as part of the will from the one-third); if he intended literally 'you are Allah,' then nothing is required because it is meaningless. The key is the testator's intention.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Manumission; freeing a slave.
نية (niyyah): Intention; the decisive factor in interpreting ambiguous legal acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, if a testator says to his slave 'You are Allah' in his will, what determines the legal outcome?
A) The literal meaning of the phrase.
B) The testator's intention.
C) The slave's request.
D) The executor's choice.

Key Takeaway
When an ambiguous expression is used in a will, the testator's intention is the decisive factor; the phrase 'You are Allah' only frees the slave if manumission was intended.

Will by Gesture (Al-Wasiyyah bil-Isharah)
Ruling on validity of a will by gesture with internal Hanafi disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the validity of a will made by gesture and explain the operative ruling (mufta bihi) according to later authorities.

Core Idea
A will by gesture is valid if clearly understood; disagreement exists within the Hanafi school.

This lesson examines the validity of a will (wasiyyah) made by a person who cannot speak due to illness and resorts to gestures. The Hanafi school has an internal difference of opinion: some scholars, like Ibn Muqatil, personally permit it but note that the majority of earlier Hanafi authorities (as-habuna) do not. Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi, a later authority, permits it provided the gesture is clearly understood. Understanding this ruling is crucial for advising on end-of-life bequests when speech is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The act is a will made by a person unable to speak due to weakness. The condition for validity according to those who permit it is that the gesture (isharah) must be clearly understood (mufhimah).
2
State the disagreement
Ibn Muqatil personally finds it permissible (jai'iz) but reports that the majority of our companions (as-habuna) do not. Abu al-Layth holds that if the gesture is understood, it is permissible.
The term 'as-habuna' here likely refers to the early Hanafi authorities including Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
Later practice may follow Abu al-Layth's view because it accommodates necessity (darurah) and allows the testator's intent to be fulfilled. This view is relied upon in fatwa.
Authority: Fatawa Abi al-Layth, a later Hanafi work.
4
Apply the ruling
In a scenario where a terminally ill patient cannot speak but nods clearly to indicate a bequest, a mufti applying Abu al-Layth's view would consider the will valid.
The gesture must be unmistakable and the testator must be incapable of speech due to illness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوصية تارة تكون بالألفاظ وتارة تكون بالإشارة المفهمة . قال في فتاوى أبي الليث : مريض أوصى وهو لا يقدر على الكلام لضعفه فأشار برأسه يعلم منه أنه يعتمد قال ابن مقاتل : تجوز وصيته عندي ولا تجوز عند أصحابنا، وكان الفقيه أبر الليث يقول : إذا فهم منه الإشارة يجوز.
And the will is sometimes by words and sometimes by an understandable sign. He said in Fatawa Abi al-Layth: A sick person made a will while he was unable to speak due to weakness, and he gestured with his head by which it is known that he intends. Ibn Muqatil said: His will is permissible according to me, but it is not permissible according to our companions. And the faqih Abu al-Layth used to say: If a gesture is understood from him, it is permissible.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of a will made by gesture when the testator cannot speak due to illness. Ibn Muqatil personally considers it valid, but he reports that the majority of our Hanafi scholars (as-habuna) do not permit it. However, the faqih Abu al-Layth (Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi) holds that if the gesture is clearly understood, it is permissible. There is a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school on this point. The relied-upon view may be that of Abu al-Layth, as it allows for necessity.

Key Terms
الإشارة المفهمة (al-isharat al-mufhimah): An understandable sign or gesture that conveys the testator's intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A terminally ill patient cannot speak but gestures by nodding his head when asked if he bequeaths his house to charity. The gesture is clearly understood. What is the ruling according to Abu al-Layth?
A) The will is invalid because words are required.
B) The will is permissible if the gesture is understood.
C) The will is valid only if written.
D) The will is invalid because the testator did not say 'I bequeath'.

Key Takeaway
A will made by gesture is valid in the Hanafi school according to the later preferred view (Abu al-Layth) provided the gesture is clearly understood and the testator is unable to speak due to illness.

Validity of a General Instruction to Execute a Written Will
Dispute over validity of general will instruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a valid and invalid will based on the clarity of charitable intent, and state the Hanafi position on testamentary instructions.

Core Idea
A vague instruction to execute a written will is invalid unless it explicitly indicates charitable intent.

In this passage, we explore a nuanced point in Hanafi wasiyyah (testamentary) law: when a testator writes a will and simply says "execute what is in this book," is that sufficient? The early Hanafi authorities differ. Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl argues that such a general instruction is invalid because the writing could be for any purpose—including non-charitable ones. However, if the testator adds a phrase clearly indicating charity (like "for charity and goodness"), the will becomes valid because the intent of qurbah (seeking closeness to Allah) is evident. This lesson will walk you through the reasoning and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Initial Ruling
The act is the testator writing a will and then issuing a verbal instruction to execute it. According to Fatawa Abi al-Layth, this general instruction is sufficient to execute the will.
This is the starting point: the will is executed simply by the instruction.
2
Apply Abu Bakr's Critique
Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl objects: the instruction is invalid because the written will might be for both rich and poor recipients—meaning it is not exclusively charitable. In Hanafi law, a will must be for a valid purpose (like charity) to be enforceable against the heirs.
The key flaw is the lack of specification of charity.
3
Identify the Exception for Charitable Intent
If the testator adds a phrase that clearly indicates charity (e.g., 'for charity and goodness' in Persian), then the will becomes valid. Such wording shows intent for qurbah.
The addition of a charity phrase transforms the ruling from batil to ja'iz.
4
Consider the Linguistic Objection
Ali ibn al-Hasan al-Sughdi notes that the Persian phrase used in the text is not from their language, so he cannot confirm its meaning. This highlights the importance of clear, understandable wording.
Ambiguous language can invalidate even a charitable will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : إذا كتب وصيته ثم قال أنفذوا وما في هذا الكتاب تنفذ وصيته ؛ هكذا ذكر في كتاب الشهادات قال الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل : هو باطل لأن هذا يكون للأغنياء والفقراء جميعاً، ولو قال ست ورمران مرروان کسد كانت الوصية جائزة لأن هذا اللفظ يراد به القربة. وقال الإمام علي بن الحسن السغدي : قوله وإن كسد ليس من لساننا فلا أعرف هذا
And in Fatawa Abi al-Layth: If he wrote his will and then said 'Execute what is in this book,' his will is executed. Thus it is mentioned in the Book of Testimony. Sheikh Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: It is invalid because this would be for the rich and the poor all together. And if he said 'for charity and for goodness' [?] the will is permissible because this wording is intended for seeking closeness to Allah. And Imam Ali ibn al-Hasan al-Sughdi said: His saying 'and if it is unsaleable' is not from our language, so I do not know this.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage contains a dispute about a testator who writes a will and then says 'Execute what is in this book.' Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl argues this is invalid because the writing could be for both rich and poor recipients (i.e., not necessarily charitable). However, if the testator adds a phrase indicating charity (like 'for charity and goodness'), then the will is valid because it shows a religious intent. The commentator Ali ibn al-Hasan al-Sughdi objects that the phrase used by the testator (the garbled Persian) is not from our language, so he cannot give a ruling on it.

Key Terms
القربة (al-qurbah): Seeking closeness to Allah; an act of piety and charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl, why is a general instruction to 'execute what is in this book' invalid?
A) The will is not witnessed.
B) The instruction could apply to both rich and poor recipients, lacking exclusive charity.
C) The testator must specify the exact amounts.
D) The instruction is only valid if in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
A vague instruction to execute a written will is invalid unless it explicitly indicates charitable intent (qurbah).
